In his lectures, Tolkien argued that the Hengest of "The Fight at Finnsburg" and of "Beowulf" was an historical rather than a legendary figure and that these works record episodes from an orally composed and transmitted history of the Hengest named in the "Anglo-Saxon Chronicle." This view has gained acceptance from a number of medieval historian and Anglo-Saxon scholars both since Tolkiens initial lectures and since the publication of this posthumous collection. Tolkiens lectures describe what he called the "Jutes-on-both-sides theory", which was his explanation for the puzzling occurrence of the word "ēotenas" found in "Beowulf". Tolkien read the word as "Jutes", and theorized that the fight was a purely Jutish feud, and Finn and Hnæf were simply caught up by circumstance.
